0375703764|5.0|This book is completely enthralling...gripping.<br /><br />I'm merely writing this review to give potential readers a chance to understand why [I believe] any negative reviewers are either 1) dead wrong about the fact that this story is not at all horrifying or \\"scary,\\" as they expected, or 2) are reading the book completely wrong.<br /><br />This book is not a book in such a way that you read from the first page to last, where the story begins and ends, and that's simply all there is to it. The book serves as a medium for not only telling multiple, complex stories, but for allowing emotion and feeling to be expressed in countless different ways, what with the formatting and such.<br /><br />I'll admit, and I'll warn you, it's a difficult read. The best way to go about it is to read it straight through, then read it again.<br /><br />There are underlying connections, there are various passages that are in fact coded and provide clues to the questions that to this day still remain unanswered, even by the most thorough cult reader. This book is not just a story. It doesn't take Danielewski ten years to just tell a story. And he's not giving in to straightforward questions about the true meaning(s) of the book itself...only to vaguely imply that the readers need to figure it out for themselves.<br /><br />If you're looking for a book that's \\"horrifying\\" in a Stephen King-esque way, and this one catches your eye, you will be disappointed.<br />This book is indeed horrifying, but only in such a way that once (or \\"if,\\" more like) you are able to begin tapping into all of the different underlying currents hidden within the medium, you will become just as obsessed about finding the answers as the characters in the story are fanatically, and sometimes depressingly, obsessed about finding their own answers that you, the reader, have to delve enough to figure out, as well. Because of that, putting this book down at night will not change the fact that you'll spend countless hours thinking about it.<br /><br />The fact that not knowing what's real and what's not, what's implied and what's deeply and artistically hidden within the pages, is horrifying enough to keep you entrenched for months, if not years.<br /><br />If you're looking for a fun, quick summer read, this is not for you.<br />If you're looking for something that will blow your mind (but only if you allow it to) and can potentially change your life and the way you approach it, this is truly an unrivaled masterpiece.

1469984202|5.0|Wool Omnibus is a haunting saga of people living out their entire lives in an underground structure called a silo, with no knowledge of life beyond their cloistered existence. The people live this way as a result of some undefined apocalypse, an event which poisoned the atmosphere so that life outside the silo is impossible.<br /><br />Originally written as a series of short stories revolving around this central theme, the Omnibus Edition brings the first five of them together under one book cover. Combined, the stories span over 500 pages. This isn't a quick read, but Hugh Howey writes well enough that it's neither bothersome nor boring to get through it.<br /><br />His writing style is descriptive enough that scenes come alive in the imagination. From everyday things like the imperfections of welds in a handrail, to extraordinary things like bubbles of air that float up through standing water to collect beneath a metal stair. Tastes and smells and sounds are sometimes real enough to linger after the book cover is closed.<br /><br />His development of the characters is deep, but gradual enough that it doesn't bog down the story line. Backstory on a person is presented slowly. As in real life, the reader gets to know a character over time. By the end of the book, the main characters are fully understood, and Howey does an admirable job of letting a reader make up their own mind as to what is \\"good\\" and \\"bad\\" in a person.<br /><br />His highest accomplishment, I think, is his depiction of the stratified organization of life in this confined existence, the clear lines that separate the lower levels from those considered more elite in society. He points out the difficulty in governing these varied peoples, the burden of protecting them from the poisoned atmosphere outside, and manages to do so without sounding preachy and political.<br /><br />I'm not a real Science Fiction fan, but it's easy to see how Wool has become a favorite of those that are. It's the kind of tale that makes a person think... about the direction that our own society is headed, how our own elected officials view themselves as the ruling class with the general unwashed populace as their subjects.<br /><br />Moreover, this is a self-published work that consistently rates as a top seller. As such, it is a ray of hope for any aspiring novelist entering the crazy crowded world of self-publishing, an inspiration to push on to do their best work.<br /><br />Well done, Mr. Howey. Well done.e that makes a person think... about the direction that our own society is headed, how our own elected officials view themselves as the ruling class with the general unwashed populace as their subjects.<br /><br />Moreover, this is a self-published work that consistently rates as a top seller. With very little to offer Anne in the way of money or property, she was persuaded by her family members and closest confidante, Lady Russell, to turn Frederick down.  Now, eight years later, Frederick has returned a rich man with half of the women in the county falling at his feet - and Anne has lived to bitterly regret her decision.<br /><br />Jane Austen's final-completed novel, PERSUASION, is one of her most mature and pragmatic works.  She cleverly plays with the pre-established pattern of her earlier stories by having the characters already in complete possession of their feelings for one another. In \\"Persuasion\\", the love story has already taken place but failed, and the challenge now is how to bring the lovers back together again.<br /><br />The BBC's 1995 film of \\"Persuasion\\" (starring Amanda Root and Ciaran Hinds) is a great favourite for many (although I've never completely warmed to it), but this latest 2007 attempt - originally part of a new season of Jane Austen adaptations for ITV - gives a refreshing new interpretation on the story.  I appreciate the efforts of Simon Burke of trying to give the character of Anne something of a backbone (this is especially evident in the final scene where Anne runs through the streets of Bath after having read Frederick's letter).<br /><br />Sally Hawkins is fast-establishing herself as one of the new \\"go-to\\" leading ladies of British period drama (after \\"Fingersmith\\" and \\"Twenty Thousand Streets Under the Sky\\"). B000AYNFWG|5.0|When I first saw Federico Fellini's 1960 classic, LA DOLCE VITA about 25 years ago, I immediately realized I was being exposed to a great film. Its iconic imagery and the intimacy it elicits despite the scope of its story left a deep impression, making a Fellini admirer out of me for life.<br /><br />LA DOLCE VITA - \\"the sweet life\\"- is a profound, eye filling, episodically structured film that follows jaded journalist Marcello in his quest to discover something of meaningful, lasting value in life. His search leaves him disillusioned when the people and things he admires and respects don't live up to his estimation of them.<br /><br />Marcello Mastroianni, the Italian equivalent of Cary Grant, is brilliant as the complex, idealistic hero. Anita Ekberg is also great in her signature role, as a glamorous movie star whose carefree lifestyle masks a troubled relationship with her abusive actor/boyfriend, played by Lex Barker. Yvonne Furneaux does well as Marcello's possessive, neurotic girlfriend unable to tolerate his aimless existence, and Alain Cuni is quite effective as Marcello's scholarly, introspective friend whose tragic fate marks the turning point in the journalist's own outlook on life.<br /><br />The film's most famous scene is of Anita Ekberg's sensual cavorting in Rome's Trevi Fountain. Other memorable moments are the opening shots of a helicopter transporting a huge statue of Christ high above Rome, Mastroianni and Ekberg ascending a winding staircase to the dome of St. Peter's Cathedral, a large crowd gathering in the rain awaiting an appearance by the Blessed Mother, and the final scene at a beach where a group of hedonists are contemplating a bellied up sea creature.<br /><br />Though tame by today's standards, LA DOLCE VITA was highly controversial when it came out, and even considered pornographic by the Catholic Church. The film was the first one to accurately depict the paparazzi, who were just beginning to emerge in the media, and the theme of tabloid sensationalism is the thread that ties all the vignettes together. This helped make LA DOLCE VITA one of the most relevant, influential films of its time, one that reflected the changing attitudes of European society at the dawn of a new decade. It received the Grand Jury Prize at the 1960 Cannes Film Festival, and has since been regarded as a cinematic landmark and Fellini's masterpiece.<br /><br />LA DOLCE VITA is available in an appropriately grand, 3 Disc Deluxe Collector's Edition that features an immaculately restored, widescreen transfer of the film. There's a smorgasbord of supplemental material that includes an introduction by director Alexander Payne, an insightful commentary by critic Richard Schickel, a collection of Fellini T.V. shorts, interviews with Fellini, Mastroianni and Ekberg, a musical montage of Cinecitta Studio, a documentary on composer Nino Rota, a discussion on LA DOLCE VITA themes, and footage with the film's screenwriter, Tullio Pinelli. A 40 page illustrated booklet, five 5\\"x7\\" photos, and an 11\\"x17\\" poster round out this sumptuous package.<br /><br />Even though life may not always be so sweet, a classic movie like LA DOLCE VITA can sure add some spice to it.<br /><br />My highest recommendation.bloid sensationalism is the thread that ties all the vignettes together. This helped make LA DOLCE VITA one of the most relevant, influential films of its time, one that reflected the changing attitudes of European society at the dawn of a new decade. B0072GPPNC|3.0|Firstly, I think it might be appropriate to share what \\"War Horse\\" is not.  It is not \\"Saving Private Ryan in World War I\\".  The realism and horror of war shown in Private Ryan, and for that matter Schindler's List, is not evident here.  No, the Great War of this film harkens further back into Spielberg's past, perhaps most closely akin to \\"Empire of the Sun\\".  Like the prison camp of Empire, this WWI is ... I'm not sure of the right word. Sanitized? Idealized?  No man's land is full of mud, but not of rats and body parts, and there's no incessant noise from shelling.  When an artillery shell does hit a trench full of men, bodies are thrown about whole.  Even in the much-talked about scene (minor spoiler!) of the horse trapped in the razor wire, there is very little blood, especially not on the face, and the horse doesn't \\"scream\\" in terror, a sound often mentioned in war memoirs as being the most horrifying sound on a battlefield.<br /><br />It will sound like a criticism to start a review this way, but that was not my intention.  I think Spielberg deliberately toned down the horror of war (and by all accounts, the WWI trenches were more horrifying than the Normandy beaches) because those horrors would not necessarily register with a horse.  Would a horse recognize a disembodied arm?  Would it care about rats crawling in No Man's Land?  Unlikely.  Therefore, this really is the story of the titular War Horse (named, innocently, Joey).  We are meant to watch Joey.  We are meant to see things through Joey's eyes.  In one telling scene (another minor spoiler!) of a cavalry charge, we see large numbers of horses go careening through the enemy lines without their riders, who have been shot off.  Only briefly do we see any dead horses, and then they look like they might be sleeping.  In fact, all parts of the film are almost stereotyped - the cast seem almost to a man (and woman) to overact just a little.  This is clearly a deliberate choice of the director, if only because Emily Watson is unimpeachable in her talents, and she's just as bad (or good!) as the rest.  Now consider the cinematography - Janusz Kaminsky's pastoral settings of rural England and France (behind the lines) are bold over-saturated colours of yellows, browns, and greens.  Presumably this represents the ideal setting for Joey.  In contrast the war scenes, where Joey is forced to pull artillery, surrounded by death and destruction, the palate is completely washed out grey.  There is none of the usual Kaminsky subtlety here, again a deliberate use of colour to emphasize mood more in keeping with a simple horse's point of view than that of an Oscar-winning DP.  Even John Williams's score is often too bold, with brassy, upbeat rythms that are compulsively musical, not what you'd expect for a Great War film.<br /><br />The film is skillfully made.  But is it a \\"good\\" film?  I suspect opinions will vary widely.  At one end, the horse's \\"acting\\" is a truly astonishing achievement.  Even so, for a Spielberg film, for me it falls somewhere behind his true classics (from Jaws through Raiders of the Lost Ark to Saving Private Ryan).  I don't think it ever really pulled me in completely.  This might partly be because of its episodic nature - Joey is the only constant, he moves from owner-to-owner right up to the last 15 minutes of the film.  In that last 15 minutes there are some reappearances of former owners (I will not say which ones!), but it's not enough.  True, the very end (the last 2 minutes, say) are emotionally moving and even uplifting, but with a cloying sentimentality - surely a deliberate choice - that seems manipulative in hindsight.  Again, perhaps like Empire of the Sun, this film hovers at the boundry of adult fare and \\"family\\" fare - not wanting to go too far into realism, but too much for the kids to enjoy.<br /><br />Overall it's a good film, but not one that I would compulsively watch if it came on TV late at night.  Perhaps I'm just too much a productof the \\"cynical era\\" to be able to totally enjoy a thickly troweled sentiment, no matter how skillfully made it is (and \\"thickly troweled\\" is rather cynical of me, isn't it?). In a rating system of 5 stars, I find it falls in the middle of the 3-star and 4-star range.  So why did I go with 3/5?  Because of the Blu-ray extras.  There are very few here.  A roundtable with a few of the actors and producers, a 3-minute short on the extras, and that's it.  I, for one, would love to have seen how the movie was created - especially the scenes of the horses at the front.  Was it a combination of computer graphics and live-action?  If so, it was totally seamless.  If it was all live action, how could you possibly get a horse to do what they did and still have healthy, living horses at the end of the shoot?

0805095535|5.0|An eclectic collection of miscellaneous thoughts, Winter Journal is exactly what the title asserts.  Always brutally honest, Auster reflects upon his sixty-three years thus far, paying special attention to the many trials and tribulations his body has withstood throughout its lifetime, the myriad places he has called home, those he has loved over the years, and (though not as explicitly as some would have you believe) his mother.<br /><br />While this all sounds rather pedestrian, I assure you, Auster renders it fascinating.  Of course, it helps to be an Auster fan where this book is concerned.  As a work of nonfiction, it reveals much about the man and offers insight into his fiction.  However, even if this is one's first encounter with Auster, the expert writing and vivid description will titillate.<br /><br />Furthermore, and I can't emphasize it enough, Auster's honesty is astounding.  I'm amazed at what he's willing to divulge about himself, especially because this is a man who doesn't need to take chances anymore.  His name could sell his book alone.  Fortunately, Auster is always the inventor, forever the innovator, and so he invites us into his life and discusses things I wouldn't tell my best friend.  Perhaps this is why he has become so successful over time - he has no fear when it comes to the written word.<br /><br />To read this book is to experience Auster's life itself, and I can offer no author a greater compliment than that.<br /><br />~Scott William Foley, author of Andropia

In the capitol district, everything is fine, but elsewhere, it's a sub-sub-standard of hand-to-mouth living.  Each district is responsible for a specific service to the country: providing coal, manufacturing, agriculture, etc.  To keep the citizens entertained, each year the capitol district hosts the Hunger Games.  Participants are selected at random, with one boy and one girl chosen from each district.  We follow the fate of the twelve, focusing on the pair from District 12.  They are very close friends Katniss, daughter of a miner killed in an explosion, and Peeta, son of a baker.  But, according to the rules of the game, only one may survive.  The dozen are placed in a specially developed arena to battle each other to the death, leaving only one survival.  The weapons include bows and arrows, slingshots, spears, rocks, anything that will make for a gory death. That's the fiction part.  The science part entails the various ways the nation has of watching the participants on TV.  Through technology, each of the 12 is continually monitored.  At the end of the day, the pictures of those who have deceased during the day are projected onto the sky so the combatants know how they stand. Further, the game directors can, at will, change the weather conditions to make life even more difficult.  Also, from time to time, support for an individual participant is parachuted to the one in need. That's just the bare-bones description of what goes on here. 145162445X|5.0|This is a wonderful and masterfully written book by one of the world renowned experts in the field of cosmology who has made fundamental contributions to the field. It informs the reader (in a very engaging way) about cutting edge research in fundamental theoretical physics and cosmology. It is very well emphasized in the book that some of the advances have been experimental (like Hubble's discovery about the expansion of the universe and the more recent discovery of the accelerated expansion of the universe, the so-called Dark Energy) while some have been theoretical (theories like inflation and multiverse).<br /><br />The author starts with historical milestones in astronomy and cosmology. It is refreshing to see an author explain in popular science books of this kind, how for example large astronomical distances are measured or observations about the geometry of the universe made. The book does a fantastic job in explaining how we know what we do know about our universe, observationally. This is the focus of the first few chapters of the book and they are really easy and fun to read and very informative.<br /><br />Throughout the book, Krauss (who has also written other amazing science books like 'Quantum Man' and 'The Physics of Star Trek') elaborately explains key theoretical concepts. All the ideas presented in the book are standard conventional wisdom in the theoretical physics community and there are NO controversial theoretical things in the book. And that is worth emphasizing. Theoretical ideas are by definition subject to experimental and observational scrutiny. And the author is well aware of that and the reader gets the sense that he is being cautious and honest about this fact in the book.<br /><br />Krauss isn't making the point that it is definitively established that the universe arose out of nothing. Instead, he very carefully and with full caution and attention to all the pitfalls of a theoretical (but not verified by observation) argument is saying that it is now possible in theoretical physics to conceive of mathematically and logically consistent theories that predict a universe very much like the one we live in, but that arose out of 'nothing', meaning no matter (and perhaps even no space) to start with.<br /><br />The very definition of 'nothing' is unambiguously emphasized and explained in the book. Krauss painstakingly and beautifully explains to the reader difficult concepts like quantum field theory and what we mean by 'nothing' in physics. There are several whole chapters devoted to 'Nothing'. It is very clearly explained what physicists mean by the term, why they think (based on observations of quantum phenomenon over the past 100 years) that this definition of 'nothing' ought to be the correct technical definition of the word and how with advances in physics, we have verified some of these ideas against experiments. Basically, 'nothing' is meant to mean complete empty space, that is no matter or light or any forces. However, as explained in the book, quantum mechanics has something deep to say about this empty state. And in fact it says this 'nothing' is actually unstable. And through this unstable 'nothing', theoretically at least, it is possible for a universe to pop onto existence and it is well explained in the book how. And this is the essential point of the book. Through 'Nothing', a universe with very much the characteristics of our universe can arise.<br /><br />The Afterword by Richard Dawkins is very good and recaps in a very broad sense the essence of the book.<br /><br />Overall the book is one of its kind and is very well written, extremely lucid in its explanations of difficult concepts and a refreshing popular science book, where one actually learns a lot of physics.

0307279189|4.0|If you loved to run and your feet hurt, what would you do?<br /><br />Chances are that you would see a podiatrist and follow his instructions.<br /><br />And what would reporter Christopher McDougall or Chuck Norris do, when the feedback of their podiatrists didn't seem appropriate?<br /><br />Go to the friggin' forgotten lands of Mexico, risk their lives in drug traffickers' territory, meet the legendary Tarahumara indian tribes... And come back to tell us the story.<br /><br />(yeah, I'm wrong about Chuck Norris, as everyone knows that Chuck Norris feet would never hurt)<br /><br />The final result is absolutely wonderful and addictive. One of the most pleasant books I've read in the past few months and I strongly recommend you to give up any reading laziness you may potentially have in the moment and give it a go. Reading this book is definitely breezier than completing a marathon in a hot sunny Sunday (well, this is not much help if you're already a long-distance runner).<br /><br />The story has so many freakantastic elements that, in the beginning, it almost felt like I was reading a fiction book - I had bought it a few months before I actually started to read it, so when I picked it up I didn't remember the summary and reviews I had seen back then. The scenario and the way the story develops are simply surreal. The characterization of the each of its participants is also done in a captivating way (they're probably captivating in the real world as well, but the author still has a merit anyway), and you can easily see yourself in the place of some of them. Especially if you have any mild relationship with the running sport, you will be cheering along and feeling the drama. Caballo Blanco, the mythic figure who one day decided that there shouldn't be a best thing in life other than simply run through Mexican Copper Canyons. Barefoot Ted, the easy-going guy who could barely stop talking during the whole book. Scott Jurek, the vegan ultramarathon runner who was the real challenger of the Gods of Running in their own sacred territory. Billy and Jenn, the two cranky youngster heads who are spontaneous at the point of going to a heavy-booze-drinking-fest just days before the great-ultimate-epic-running-battle against the legendary and invincible Tarahumara men. If you get emotionally involved in stories like me, you may even get angry at both of them at some point (\\"Come on, you guys really expect to complete a marathon in such a state?\\"). I remember myself on every time I have a Saturday drinking-fest of my own,  in which case I can barely help my girl to wash the dishes after Sunday lunch (ok, ok, I admit it, maybe this is just a lame excuse).<br /><br />Even though I'm not a marathon warrior such as those people, running shorter 6-9 km distances 3-4 times a week is something that I really love and can't live without. In that sense, one of the great things about this book is how it just simply nails what's really wonderful about running. In some of its best and most emotional excerpts, I found myself smiling and saying \\"YEAH, THAT'S IT!\\", and then almost getting up to run like a child out there. So if you're in need of some help to start or to re-establish a running routine, I assure you that no psychologist or supporting friend will be better than Christopher McDougall (despite the fact that supporting friends can be convincing and fun to have around, the author must have spent hours crafting some well-thought sentences in here). If you're curious to see what's so wonderful about running, but at the same time are too lazy to read the book, do a Google search on 'scott jurek arnulfo quimare' and look very hard at the pictures of the results, trying to absorb all the essence of what Chris is trying to convey (we'll see if a picture can really be worth a thousand words this time). Yeah, it's Scott Ultra-Jurek and tarahumara Arnulfo Quimare having one of the best time of their lives<br /><br />So if you think the book is so wonderful, so awesome, so perfect, why the 4-stars?<br /><br />Well, because for me only God is worthy of 5-stars.<br /><br />Just kidding.<br /><br />Actually the detractor is that, along the narrative, you also get as a special bonus some scientific incursions on various running & health-related issues which, in my opinion, were not on par with the rest of the book. I'm not even biased when I say this, as I actually do not disagree with most of them. I just think that the author was taking sides on very controversial issues without all the necessary fundamentals and warnings. That means bringing insufficient data to the debate and reaching conclusions with potentially flawed and/or incomplete analysis. In some occasions, he also gives recommendations which are more simplistic and incomplete than they should, which increases the risk that the less cautious reader makes decisions that may even hurt his health.<br /><br />Take the whole barefoot vs running shoes debate, for example. I would say that, given that this was a book about the epic battle of America vs the almighty Rar&aacute;muri, the analysis of the benefits of barefoot running were pretty adequate, having the proper length and depth within the book. However, when the author recommends readers to try the new approach, he simply ignores that the transition to barefoot should be slow, REALLY slow! If you're not the kind of person who is willing to take the time to do much further research on the web and books and simply decide to follow the author, chances are high that you'll be injuring yourself BIG TIME! In case you want a first free tip to start out, search for the Harvard website on barefoot running, which has many helpful videos and visual explanations on the subject.<br /><br />I must admit that, initially, I thought that McDougall was being way too biased on this subject. After all, he was praising barefoot and throwing darts at Nike in such an exaggeratedly energetic way. At some point I was totally convinced that this guy had received some really bad service at a Nike Store or at Nike's call center, and was making use of the privilege of being the writer of a book about Tarahumara runners to claim revenge on this evil shoe company. Not that I'm not judging him. I would probably do the same too if the staff at the Nike Store in Rio weren't so nice at me (yeah Vanessa, if you're reading this, beware).<br /><br />However, that initial suspicion abruptly changed due to an unexpected coincidence just after I finished the book... for the first time since I started running, MY GODDAMN KNEE STARTED TO HURT!!!! I'm not a fool, of course this was some weird kind of indigenous spell cast by McDougall, just to make sure that I wouldn't go out there disagreeing with him for free. Due to a  knee inflammation, I spent a few weeks with some pain everytime I put a higher load on my knees. This meant that I got very sensitive to the kind of shoes I was wearing and the kind of foot strike I was doing.<br /><br />And what did I find out? McDougall was right! The most knee-hurting shoes of all are the formal shoes I use for work, followed by my flip-flops. Still, for 1 or 2 weeks, the only kind of footwear that didn't really hurt me was wearing no footwear at all while doing the jazzy forefoot strike of our caveman ancestors. Of course this meant that my next step would be following Barefoot Ted's barefoot steps, and order my own ridiculous-looking Vibrams. They're even more ridiculous in Brazil, because they are not popular at all here yet, and everybody at my neighbourhood keeps staring at my frog-like-feet while I'm doing the grocery. Damn your wizardry, McDougall!<br /><br />Then comes the whole vegan diet thing, advocated by our audacious book hero Scott Jurek. I'm really not going into the whole \\"vegan vs non-vegan\\" debate, which is extensive and not adequate to this review - besides the fact that I'm no MD, just a Brazilian economist who happened to have been a vegan for 8 years and gone back to chicken meat a few years ago. I'll just point that, before praising all the vegan benefits alongside our ultra-hero , the author could at least have posed a few pertinent questions, such as \\"What are the long-term yet-unseen implications of this diet to the health of the person?\\", \\"Could it be that the body of an ultra-runner adapts to this diet in a different way vs the casual athlete?\\" After all, Scott Jurek could be and feel healthy potentially because he's an ultra-athlete, but that doesn't necessarily mean that a non-ultra-runner will feel as healthy with the same diet. As for the long-term issue, from my own experience I can tell how a vegan diet can make you feel awesome and invincible (assuming you're not running against the Tarahumara, of course) in the short-term due to the whole \\"body-cleansing\\" effect. However, the lack of certain substances (folic acid, B-12, among others) in a vegan diet combined with overuse of soy products may potentially impair some aspects your health once you get older to your `60s and `70s (eg. cognition, bone health). But of course we're not going to see this risk materialize in our young Scott Jurek!<br /><br />Anyways, besides my heads-up on those issues, I'll highlight again what I said in the beginning of the review: this is an AWESOME book, whose 300 pages I devoured in just a few days, even though I normally have a 12-hour workday. I just couldn't let go of it! So if you're bothered by the scientific flaws I mentioned above, just skip them and have a good time enjoying the amazing America vs Tarahumara racing story. Go get the book! And go running! Barefoot.

We've gotten too sophisticated with all the computer generated special effects. Yes, they are incredible, but take a step back into the early 70's when specific effects required a lot of labor and often danger.<br />PRODUCER/DIRECTOR IRWIN ALLEN was famous in the 60's for his sci-fi fantasty TV shows like LOST IN SPACE, THE TIME TUNNEL and LAND OF THE LOST. He took a different career path inthe 1970's with the disaster epic THE POSEIDON ADVENTURE to become the king of disaster films. He followed that huge success with THE TOWERING INFERNO.<br />The world's tallest and news skyscraper is about to celebrate his official opening with a gala party. Many members of high society and the political world will be attending the party on the very top floor. Unfortunately, corners have been cut to save costs and many safety features are compromised. A small fire turns into THE TOWERING INFERNO.<br />Again, you need to watch the special features to truly understand the undertaking it was to film this movie. Fire is unpredictable and yes, stunt actors were actually on fire in several scenes. The bravery of this cast and crew is amazing.<br />Speaking of the cast, another fun part of this film is seeing actors who were in their heyday in the early 1970's along with famous actors like Fred Astaire and Jennifer Jones from the past. It's easy to draw comparisons with 9/11 and people trapped in a burning skyscraper. Fortunately, the people in our story have some chance for survival.<br />Back to the special effects, it's still amazing to watch although some effects look cheesy by today's standards. Again, we have to remember that there were limitations on how realistic special effects could be. The crew of THE TOWERING INFERNO did an amazing job.<br />This film is a step back in time. It's not as realistic in many ways as modern films, but considering the resources that were avalaible at the time, THE TOWERING INFERNO is an amazing achievement.

1469984202|5.0|While this Omnibus feels like a novel, it was originally published as a series of five novellas -- and self-published at that. But don't let that scare you, it's better written, better edited, and far more engaging than 95% of New York published SciFi. In some ways a throwback, in some ways very modern, Wool is a contained (in both the literary and literal way) post apocalyptic tale in the mould of Larry Niven or A Canticle for Leibowitz. Technically this is an ARC story, about an isolated world built to survive a destroyed environment. The people in Wool live in a 148 floor Silo in the ground. To leave is to die.<br /><br />The book is written in first person tight from multiple points of view. Each section has a fairly clear POV character and the narrative voice highlights their perspective. There is no single protagonist. The first novella is basically a short story introducing the world and ending with the usual short story twist. It did very well on Amazon and the author wrote a second (using a minor character from the first as the POV) and then a third which essentially transitions to the larger story's most important character (Jules). It's very much to Howey's credit that this serial construction does not feel artificial. The works holds up both individually as a cohesive and epic novel.<br /><br />Several things lead to the overall excellence: The characters are well developed and mostly likable (the main villain is a little thin, but interesting enough). The world is intriguing  and detailed with an appropriate pacing of reveals. There is a good amount of death and suffering in this novel and it lends a generally tragic air to the whole situation as well as the specific events. There is also a lot of tension despite what might nominally be plotting that doesn't showcase a lot of overt external conflict (in the first 2-3 books). Basically it's just very good.<br /><br />The silo is well thought out. My  only real beef at a technical level (and this doesn't distract from the book at all) is the unlikelyhood that such a contained ecosystem (in all senses of the word) could remain so functional over several centuries. On a practical level earthly eco-systems function because of the ENORMOUS quantity of solar energy constantly added. Wool features a pretty big system powered by a single main fossil-fuel generator. Maybe that's possible, and Howey's navel background lends copious verisimilitude and gritty detail, but I suspect to really make a big spaceship or ARC last a long time you'd need some really serious juice. Large scale hydroponic farming alone would require a hell of a lot of power. But as fiction, it's really well worked out.<br /><br />Additionally, at a realistic level, I'm not a big believer in the predictability of human large scale behavior (aka history), but in the context of Science Fiction like this (taking its queue from Asimov's Foundation), there's no problem. In the real world, despite the endlessly repeating basic patterns of history, no human has ever proven to be a great predictor and controller of the long term specifics.<br /><br />Another minor peeve is that the Kindle version, while well proofread, has a very unusual formatting with a tiny font oversized spacing between paragraphs. I had to jack the Kindle scale feature up several notches to even read the text. There's (currently) really no reason not to use the default font style for MOBI/EPUB body text in a novel.<br /><br />But if you like Science Fiction, post apocalyptic worlds, or just plain old good novels. Read this. Seriously, it's one of the most enjoyable speculative novels I've read in years. Bravo.<br /><br />Andy Gavin, author of The Darkening Dreamdistract from the book at all) is the unlikelyhood that such a contained ecosystem (in all senses of the word) could remain so functional over several centuries. Being an older Testament fan, I don't find the same fault in this album others may. Like I said above, maybe this isn't their most popular album but at least it's metal, which is more than I can say for a lot of bands who went thru the 90's. Van Halen was mentioned, but I didn't see anything about the Pixies or the Sound.)<br /><br />The book is set in 2045, where somehow the entire world (including every rights activist, environmentalist, and everyone else fighting for the world) has receded into the OASIS: the future's World of Warcraft on steroids, in order to escape from the problems humanity has been ignoring for this long anyways, so why stop now? While I was reading the book, if I came across a particularly grueling passage where Cline would spend pages upon pages listing information that Wade knew, my family and I would play a game: I would read the passage out loud, and they would try and force each other to listen to the passage the longest. The one part I enjoyed of this book were my mom and my sister writhing on the ground as they tried to get away while I read from the book. Even the prospect of writing this review didn't make finishing the book worth it, and this is the one essay where I wished the prompt had been \\"Did you like this book? Why or why not?\\" As it was, the message of the book is very important, but the writing itself was so horrible that I had trouble even caring that Cline had put so much effort into calling attention to the problems in the world.

0374275637|5.0|This is a book about why people do what they do (and then make up reasons after the fact). Daniel Kahnerman paints a broad canvas, moving between tightly sketched nuances and grand explanations. He delves into some of the more profound questions confronting us, and does it all with the practiced ease of good, if not great writer.<br /><br />The story (and it is a story, as I will return to) begins with his recollecting his close and departed friend Amos Tversky and concludes with two papers that they co-wrote. In between is a feast of behavioural psychology, all premised around the idea that we humans decide things sometimes through intuitive guesses that might be the right answer but to the wrong question (System One thinking- the fast thinking of the title) and at other times through working through the details (System Two thinking, the slow thinking of the title). These complementary problem solving approaches can work harmoniously for our advantage, or (it seems to often) land us in deep water.<br /><br />About three quarters of the way through the book I became conscious of when I was responding primarily with one of the two thinking systems. Sitting in an airplane on a flight between Brisbane Australia and Noumea I was well and truly in the System One space - wine served with lunch further entrenched that mode. Things 'made sense' and didn't need to be laboured over. Then, the next morning over breakfast I found myself in System Two thinking - even taking the time to re-read material that on the previous day seemed to make sense.<br /><br />Which brings me to the comment earlier that this book is really a story. System One thinking, to my mind, works best with narrative, the telling of a story. Stories forgive the lack of tightly argued cases, leaning on the use of metaphor and common sense. Kahneman's weaving of his life experience (eg., as an officer in the Israeli Army, leader of a curriculum development team) and his constant references back to his departed colleague and friend, Amos, are the makings of a story. If you took those elements out then this book would be yet another droll text on nothing very much new. But it is the interplay between the 'science' and the 'narrative' that brings this book to life. If you haven't read it do so - it is a treat. And if you have read it, dip back into a section of two - they're mostly under 20 pages - and consciously re-read them with your 'System One' (fast) and 'System Two' (slow) hats on. If you are anything like me you will realise that while the system two arguments are cogent as you would expect from a writer of this callaber, it is the system one narratives that carry the day.d colleague and friend, Amos, are the makings of a story. If you took those elements out then this book would be yet another droll text on nothing very much new. But it is the interplay between the 'science' and the 'narrative' that brings this book to life. If you haven't read it do so - it is a treat. And if you have read it, dip back into a section of two - they're mostly under 20 pages - and consciously re-read them with your 'System One' (fast) and 'System Two' (slow) hats on. If you are anything like me you will realise that while the system two arguments are cogent as you would expect from a writer of this callaber, it is the system one narratives that carry the day.

Perhaps because I enjoyed the previous two novels so much, I found Zafon's effort in The Prisoner of Heaven utterly disappointing.<br /><br />Zafon always stated that he intended the novels commencing with The Shadow of the Wind to be stand-alone works, interconnected by various characters and, most importantly, the Cemetery of Forgotten Books.  Thus, a reader could start with any one of the novels and jump into an intriguing mystery story -- chronology is not important.  Zafon previously stated the following:<br /><br />\\"I never meant to write a sequential saga, or a series of sequels of sorts. The idea is to write stories around this literary universe centered around the cemetery of forgotten books, exploring this gothic, mysterious universe through different characters and storylines. As you say, perhaps it would have been more commercialy advisable to do that, to write a straight sequel and pick up the story where we left it, but it was never my idea to do so and I think it is more interesting to play around with the narrative spaces and lines to pull the reader into a fictional universe that plays by its own rules.\\"<br /><br />Zafon has, apparently, now scrapped this idea.  Where the first two novels are marvelous mysteries in their own right (who is Julian Carax; who is the \\"boss\\"?) -- The Prisoner of Heaven is merely filler, nothing more than an explanation of elements of the first two novels and a set-up for the final novel. The Prisoner of Heaven doesn't contain a riveting mystery, it doesn't develop characters (indeed all of the major characters are persons we've met before), it doesn't add to the mysterious qualities of The Cemetery of Forgotten Books, and (most importantly) it doesn't have a satisfactory conclusion in its own right.  Rather, the novel does two things: (i) it attempts to resolve matters/mysteries from the first two books (matters and mysteries that, in my opinion, didn't need a resolution); and (ii) apparently sets up the next novel.<br /><br />With respect to the first point, the mystery surrounding The Angel's Game (whether the \\"boss\\" existed at all or whether David was indeed insane and had imagined the entire episode) was completely obliterated by this book.  Zafon sums up one of his most intriguing characters (David Martin) in the simplest and most uninteresting manner possible -- he's crazy, end of story.  Just like that, all the mystery is drained from the otherwise superb The Angel's Game.  I guess Zafon wants the reader to just forget about the epilogue to The Angel's Game or pretend the events in the epilogue never occurred, as it is completely contradicted by The Prisoner of Heaven (or, I guess, it can all be unsatisfactorily explained away simply by saying Martin is crazy).<br /><br />Likewise, now Zafon in The Prisoner of Heaven (in my opinion) reinvents both Fermin and Isabella.  Now the chance meeting of a vagabond and a young boy was always meant to happen; now Isabella's death was no accident (although the resolution to this part of the story did not occur in The Prisoner of Heaven -- I thought these books could stand on their own?).  I would have preferred not to know whether the \\"boss\\" existed or was a figment of David's imagination.  I would have preferred to believe that Fermin and Daniel met and became friends by chance, rather than some elaborate vow as a result of a prison escape straight out of the Count of Monte Cristo.  Isn't that part of what made the mysteries of The Shadow of the Wind and The Angel's Game so entertaining?  Now characters have been reformed and mystery obliterated for the sake of simply moving the story along.<br /><br />In summary, The Prisoner of Heaven felt like a very rushed attempt to capitalize on the popularity of the previous two novels.  The idea of independent, though interconnected, mystery stories located in Barcelona and centered around the Cemetery of Forgotten Books has been abandoned, as I have to assume that the fourth novel will resolve the lingering issues brought up in The Prisoner of Heaven(did I mention that the Prisoner of Heaven doesn't really contain a conclusion).<br /><br />Zafon previously stated \\"since each novel was going to be complex and difficult to write, I decided to take one at a time and see how the experiment evolved on its own in an organic way.\\"  The Prisoner of Heaven is not complex (checking in at only 280 or so pages) and did not evolve on its own (no new characters, no independent story, etc.) -- perhaps Zafon should have revisited his own words and crafted a novel that fit the style and pattern of the previous two.

B000002OX7|5.0|Much has been written about this recording coming out of the failed Lifehouse project. Set that history aside and simply take in the stunning suite of songs on this album. Track for track, the original nine songs not only make this the best Who record by a long mile, it makes it in my mind the best pure rock & roll record ever made. Yes, that's quite a statement given rock's long history but what makes this record simply sensational is that each member of the band was at his absolute creative peak. These are Townsend's greatest set of songs, whether he was rocking out at maximum R&B or writing his most soul baring, achingly beautiful songs. Daltry came into his own as a singer here, not just singing loudly but with real feeling and power. Entwistle was a beast here, practically reinventing the bass and Keith Moon was a force of nature. All of this would make it a great rock record, a landmark, but what pushes it to the very pinnacle of hard rock albums is Townsend's radical use of synthesizers. This has been covered at length but it bears repeating that no one had ever used synthesizers in this way before and the interplay between their insistent, almost primal repetitions and Moon having to coordinate his drumming is simply stunning. Tunes like Baba O'Riley, Bargain and Won't Get Fooled Again are powerhouse songs that reinvented rock. Most good rock bands would give their eye teeth to have this record as their greatest hits package, let alone a single record. The Who never rocked harder or laid their soul bare in the same way before or after. From start to finish this record outshines any single record the Stones ever cut. Or any other true rock band for that matter. Greatest hard rock album of all time? You bet.

I agree with many of his sentiments, but I disagree with his approach in this book. It follows that there is no valid proof for the -requirement- of a God-like entity. You cannot prove that God exists, and you cannot prove that God must exist. And since there is no valid proof for the existence (or not) of a God-like entity, your religious beliefs must be based entirely on faith.<br /><br />Therefore as a scientist I must leave God out of my math equations, and I must leave the question of God to the philosophers and theologians. B005LAIH4A|2.0|When Dr. Seuss gets serious, you don't know what you are going to get.  That's because his extremely left-wing views sometimes lead him to the truth, sometimes blind him to the truth, and sometimes just lead him astray.  Thus his story about [[ASIN:0394800893 The Sneetches]] still stands as one of the most effective condemnations of the stupidity of prejudice based upon appearance ever written, and [[ASIN:0394800877 Yertle the Turtle and Other Stories]] contains three brilliantly effective modern fables.  On the other hand Dr. Seuss' own prejudices (he was an extreme pro-abortionist) made him absolutely horrified to learn that pro-lifers were drawing the obvious conclusion from the moral of [[ASIN:0394800788 Horton Hears A Who!]]: \\"A person's a person, no matter how small,\\" by applying it to unborn children, and despite the avalanche of demands to cease and desist with lawsuits to follow, Dr. Seuss went to his grave knowing that despite his best (or worst) intentions, he had written the unofficial but extremely effective motto of the pro-life movement.  Dr. Seuss' worst book by far is [[ASIN:0394865804 The Butter Battle Book]], but in fairness to him, writing it was an act of sheer desperation.  He probably knew he was dying, and in 1984 his beloved Soviet Union (Dr. Seuss was a Communist sympathizer before being a Communist sympathizer was cool.) was also dying at the hands of President Ronald Reagan who unlike most \\"experts\\" of all political persuasions believed the Cold War was winnable.  In the end Dr. Seuss failed, Ronald Reagan succeeded, and a lot of kids were forced to read a truly subpar book because their teachers liked its politics.<br /><br />This brings us to [[ASIN:0394823370 The Lorax]], arguably Dr. Seuss' second worst book, not because the message hammered home with a sledgehammer in it was particularly evil, but rather because it was so unnecessary.  Stripped of its dramatics and anti-business bias, the primary message of The Lorax was don't only chop down and never plant the trees you depend on for your business.  Well, DUH!  American logging companies were starting to figure this out in the NINETEENTH Century!  Certainly by the time The Lorax was written, there wasn't an American logging company that wasn't planting several trees for every tree they cut down.  The secondary message was don't carelessly and thoughtlessly pollute the air and the water, and while it took longer for this message to sink in, by 1971 it really had, and the air and water were rapidly being cleaned up.  I did then and I do now have a problem with a children's story that consciously and deliberately pressures small children to solve a problem that their parents have already solved.  What next? What are you doing?<br /><br />I was cooped up in a small room with a fan, no swimming suit in sight, reading this book so eagerly that I had to cover the next page with my hand so my eyes weren't tempted to skip ahead.<br /><br />It's that good.<br /><br />The characters are so real, I forget that they are a figment of Howey's imagination. The world is so real, I could draw it.  I could make it myself.  Down to every detail.  The people are so real, I know their faults and their traits.  I can even picture their distinctive movements.  This book had my heart racing.<br /><br />I cannot remember the last time I devoured a book such as this one. The pages are bent hastily, the spine is broken, a bit of the side is torn, and there are smudges on most pages.  I took this book with me everywhere, praying for a quick moment where I could unfold the corner of a page, only to fold the next one when something came up.<br /><br />It is amazing!  I can't wait to read his other books.  I can't bring myself to read them on a kindle (sorry, Amazon) like my sister and mother do-- it was so delicious reading it on the pages, gripping the edges of the cover, and pawing at the corners when I'm ready for the next chapter.  I'd recommend buying this book immediately.<br /><br />I can't even tell you any details, paranoid I'd ruin it.  I can only tell you how amazing it is.  I shoved it into my best friend's hands once I'd returned from my vacation, and my mother asked me who would play a character in the movie.  My eyes bulged and I shook my head-- thinking the name of a character already divulgedtoo much information.  So I can only say- just read it.<br /><br />Other books I love, so maybe you can get a feel for my taste in literature:<br /><br />The Forgotten Garden<br />The Historian<br />Hunger Games<br />City of Ember<br />Frankenstein<br />A Certain Slant of Light<br /><br />-Cody Ozcan

0553801473|3.0|What I suspected was happening half way through A Feast for Crows is now confirmed with A Dance with Dragons: GRRM has fallen in love with the world he created. This is not uniquely GRRM; it happens when an author chose a particular series as the defining work to cement his legacy in the literary world (i.e. Jordan and his Wheel of Time). We've passed the \\"projected\\" half-way point in the series. So instead of tying off loose ends and bring in the story arcs for the much anticipated finale in the 7th book (the \\"projected\\" final book), he is expanding the story universe and filling in extra details to add more color and substance to the fictional world he created. A Dance with Dragons continued in the same vein as A Feast for Crows with excessive character dramatization and painfully slow pacing. To truly understand how slow the pacing had become, one needs to look at the chronology of Steven Erikson's Malazan Book of the Fallen series. Erikson completed this incredibly well-written, massive, and world-spanning 10-book series in approximately the same time it took GRRM to finish the last 2 books. For true fans that followed this series since the beginning, this book is still worth reading. It's not as incoherent and meandering as A feast for Crows. The plot does advance in A Dance with Dragons, just at a snail's pace.

B003O86FMW|5.0|While some series flop after the first book, this one certainly doesn't!  I was turned off at first by the reviews which complained about the main character, Katniss, being too whiny, weak, or indecisive in contrast with the first book.  I did not see it that way, although I understood why people were complaining of that.  I think Suzanne Collins does a good job with character continuity and deepening throughout the series and people who think that Katniss devolves into some sort of helpless Bella Swan didn't really understand her in the first place.  She is a scared 17-year-old forced to grow up way too quickly in a society which is really just trying to kill her, with hardly any support network, shoved unwillingly into a conflict bigger than herself.  Her biggest fault is probably wanting to live a semi-normal life instead of becoming the rebel leader that others are trying to mold and manipulate her into being.  Her behavior towards her two love interests, Gale and Peeta, is aggravating and perhaps selfish in that she seems unable to firmly choose one, but honestly, what 17-year-old has the emotional maturity to make such a heart-wrenching choice when she barely feels in control of her own life?  Overall I found the characters understandable, the plot gripping, and the universe that Collins creates very believable.  I tried to read as slowly as I could to savor it and still ended up finishing it in a day.  HIGHLY recommended.

B003XF1XOQ|5.0|This series is not a romance.  It is also not an action-adventure series.  This book, in particular, is a real, no-fluff portrayal of the psychological agony that comes along with conflict and great upheaval.  When I read the other reviews for this before deciding to buy it, half of them praised it for being accurate to the physical and mental ravages of war, and half of them lambasted it for being slow-moving, the characters being weak, and overall not delivering to the level of the Hunger Games.  I would have to say that the first people read the novel for what it was and the second people were simply expecting something that it isn't.  I will admit that sometimes I got really angry at Katniss for the decisions she was or wasn't making, but then I had to stop and think about how 17-year-old me would have handled the kind of pressure and manipulation she was facing.  In a society that's set up to kill you, you discover more and more that even your closest friends are trying to mold you to their purposes.  You can't trust anyone and every time you let yourself get attached, more pain and destruction comes.  Can you blame Katniss for being a mental and emotional wreck?  Yet Collins portrays her very sympathetically and not as some strong superhuman character who doesn't need relationships and affection.  Katniss is probably not a good model for feminists, but she is real.  Whether or not you agree with her course of action, you can usually understand and sympathize with why she does what she does, which is the mark of a well-developed character.<br /><br />My one critique of the book would be the hurried way in which it ends, in a huge montage of action-packed scenes that honestly seem like they were mostly just put in to tie up loose ends.  You get so involved in the characters that you feel like there's not enough closure at times, but then again, that is also a real aspect of war.  There is a big twist at the end but somehow it doesn't feel fake or staged just soit can keep you hooked all the way through.  I knew how fast I would blaze through this so I tried to read slowly and go over every passage twice, and I was still done within a weekend.  Beware, this is not a book you can easily put down!

B005LAIHY0|1.0|I think the movie's near-hysterical marketing campaign did it in more than any number of flaws and acting problems in the movie itself.  Being billed as somehow the next generation of movie-making, an epic so incredible and so beyond the medium of cinema itself, with a plot supposedly so spellbinding and powerful that it has to be kept secret until the screenings.<br /><br />And I can safely say there was almost nothing original, colossal, or paradigm-shifting in this entire movie.<br /><br />The plot is basically the next step to the Ancient Alien craze brought on by the crazies on \\"Ancient Aliens\\" on History Channel (IF ALIENS DIDN'T EXiST, HOW DID THEY BUILD EVERYTHING?!  I DON'T KNOW, THEREFORE ALIENS), by having someone rich and stupid enough front a trillion dollars for a deep space vessel to follow up on what they think to be a starmap in the form of pictographic carvings found all over the earth from far-flung, disconnected civilizations all portraying the same set of circles in the sky.<br /><br />Leaving side the obvious conjecture that maybe they all saw the same cosmic event or series of meteors rather than assuming it's somehow alien-related, the fact that this starmap leads where it does makes it a gaping plot hole, considering that the location the go to is one where the aliens clearly would never want us to ever find if we did manage to reach the technological level to find it and go to it.  Add to that the fact that it's a trillion dollar mission that could have ended up leading to nowhere if my hypothesis that it wasn't aliens was correct.<br /><br />There are no real \\"characters\\" to this story; they're little more than cardboard cut-outs with their personalities etched in their faces and speech.  Archetypes largely consisting of people with no discernable purpose beyond dying and being \\"sciencey\\".  Their only role in this movie is as \\"Meat 1\\", \\"Meat 2\\" and so on.<br /><br />Considering this is a TRILLION DOLLAR MISSION trying to find THE FIRST ABSOLUTE EVIDENCE OF LIFE OUTSIDE OF EARTH, much less ALIENS WHO CREATED US, this is facepalmingly stupid the cast of cretins they've gathered up for this mission.  I literally thought the random punk guy was some random mercenary hired on for protection, so I was genuinely shocked when they started relying on him being \\"sciencey\\" later on in the movie.  The rest of them are stupid enough to make people with even the most basic knowledge of archaeology and biology shake their heads.<br /><br />Considering once again that this is a TRILLION DOLLAR MISSION bent on finding LIVING ALIENS, I honestly thought it was either an inside joke or a jab at the people when they walk into an ancient cavern on the alien planet, unopened in 2000 years, and start TOUCHING everything, stepping everywhere, and SITTING on the artifacts.  There's a reason archaeologists use those tiny little brushes to dust off found artifacts; THEY'RE TRYING TO PRESERVE AND DOCUMENT A SITE; THEY DON'T JUMP INTO A PIT OF BONES AND START KICKING SKULLS AROUND.<br /><br />They also see fit to take off their helmets, just because the air is breathable.  Nevermind the fact that people are strongly urged to immunize just to visit another country for fear of contracting some unknown disease, these people are on a WHOLE OTHER PLANET UNTOUCHED BY HUMANS OR ANY LIFE FORM FOR TWO THOUSAND YEARS.  The TRILLION DOLLAR MISSION could've ended then and there with all of them dying of space-smallpox, and all due to their own stupidity.<br /><br />Pretty much from the moment of their arrival, the movie plays out less as THE STORY OF OUR BEGINNING and more like a typical horror movie.  I had constant instances of deja vu with this movie reminding me of scenes from \\"Alien vs Predator\\".  Even the characters had exactly the same tones and reactions as those in AVP.<br /><br />Michael Fassbender is a great actor, but his performance here is terribly uneven, though it seems more to me symptomatic of bad writing than any fault ofhis own.  His entire opening sequence of him watching Lawrence of Arabia, combing his hair like Peter O'Toole, riding his bike while shooting perfect hoops in basketball, and just walking around maintaining the ship play him up as a bright-eyed innocent, an android fully aware of what he is and giddy to learn more about the world and himself.  Then at random instances, he becomes sarcastic, dry, seemingly malevolent, even snotty, enough to arouse suspicion in a child, much less supposed scientists on a TRILLION DOLLAR MISSION.  He then pulls a temporary heel turn with a little \\"science\\" experiment that would be considered crude and reckless by Medieval standards of medicine---people DID practice quarantine back then.<br /><br />I say temporary because as the film progresses some more, this action of his not only proves to be pointless, but is subsequently dropped and ignored for the rest of the film.<br /><br />Then comes the whole moral of the story... I see a lot of positive reviews mentioning that it's okay that the story \\"doesn't explain\\" everything, or that it's not the film at fault if you \\"don't get it\\".<br /><br />Well I got it.  What I got was grade-school navel gazing hiding its own childishness behind a slick veneer of cool and hip to dismiss any criticism as \\"You just didn't get it\\".<br /><br />As much as I trash on \\"Ancient Aliens\\" on History Channel, I find the idea of paleolithic contact to be utterly thrilling, only waiting for even the slimmest shred of evidence to justify giving it further exploration.  This movie began with something like that, and close to the end, completely dropped the existential navel-gazing unaddressed to turn to the philosophical quandry of a bawling child wondering \\"Why is daddy hurting me?\\"<br /><br />From there, another gaping plothole emerges; the aliens are suddenly hostile towards us, and the humans can't understand why.  They then proceed to make up reasons that completely ignore the facts; we're BAAAAAD people!  We burn fossil fuels!  We don't recycle!  We kill each other!  We eat animals!  We invent big shooty guns and bombs!  We touch ourselves at night!<br /><br />It never gets that specific, but the \\"Why me?\\" wailing gets tired, fast, and suddenly I realized I don't care what the aliens wanted.  If they have a problem with us and are unwilling to negotiate, we can just kill them all.  They DID leave their planet full of a self-replicating biological superweapon ABANDONED for two thousand years, and then left us with the starmaps all over our planet!<br /><br />The ending is typical, and would seemingly open the door for sequels, though of the sort that would be close to nothing like this movie, and thoroughly unwanted or uncared about.  Considering they had devised this great plan for the human race, it seems downright nutty of the scientist to STILL want to meet with them and ask them \\"Why me?\\"<br /><br />As for visuals, I suppose they were impressive.  I wasn't impressed with them; CGI is cheap, fast, and easy these days.  A dozen movies are churned out with better and better visuals and plots and characters just as bad every year.  Boasting the greatest special effects with no substance to justify them will only make the movie look quaint and silly in 5-10 years when something better is done.<br /><br />I found a slight comparison with this film and \\"2001: A Space Odyssey\\" and realized it was literally the exact same basic plot:  Man finds evidence of ancient aliens, invests in a deep space mission to seek out the aliens based on their map, stuff happens, only one survivor and their pet robot.<br /><br />What separates this from \\"2001\\" is that \\"2001\\" told a story that needed no words; it was the story of us, open to all manner of interpretation, with scientists actually behaving like SCIENTISTS---Poole and Bowman kept their calm when they suspected HAL of lying to them, they casually gathered inside a space pod to discuss it, they didn't freak out and start cursing at each other or playing childish games of pretend with a damn SUPERCOMPUTER thinking they could outsmart a machine designed to read people like a pamphlet.<br /><br />In \\"Prometheus\\", they don't let you think, at least not the way you're expected to.  You're not left thinking about our beginnings, or the ancient alien concept, so much as wondering the same dumb question of \\"Why us?\\".  In fact, when we saw the movie, the only thing we kept wondering about is exactly what the alien at the prologue did and whether he knew what was happening.  I don't think that is what the filmmakers wanted.burn fossil fuels!  We don't recycle!  We kill each other!  We eat animals!  We invent big shooty guns and bombs!  We touch ourselves at night!<br /><br />It never gets that specific, but the \\"Why me?\\" wailing gets tired, fast, and suddenly I realized I don't care what the aliens wanted. Considering they had devised this great plan for the human race, it seems downright nutty of the scientist to STILL want to meet with them and ask them \\"Why me?\\"<br /><br />As for visuals, I suppose they were impressive.  I wasn't impressed with them; CGI is cheap, fast, and easy these days.  A dozen movies are churned out with better and better visuals and plots and characters just as bad every year.  Boasting the greatest special effects with no substance to justify them will only make the movie look quaint and silly in 5-10 years when something better is done.<br /><br />I found a slight comparison with this film and \\"2001: A Space Odyssey\\" and realized it was literally the exact same basic plot:  Man finds evidence of ancient aliens, invests in a deep space mission to seek out the aliens based on their map, stuff happens, only one survivor and their pet robot.<br /><br />What separates this from \\"2001\\" is that \\"2001\\" told a story that needed no words; it was the story of us, open to all manner of interpretation, with scientists actually behaving like SCIENTISTS---Poole and Bowman kept their calm when they suspected HAL of lying to them, they casually gathered inside a space pod to discuss it, they didn't freak out and start cursing at each other or playing childish games of pretend with a damn SUPERCOMPUTER thinking they could outsmart a machine designed to read people like a pamphlet.<br /><br />In \\"Prometheus\\", they don't let you think, at least not the way you're expected to.  You're not left thinking about our beginnings, or the ancient alien concept, so much as wondering the same dumb question of \\"Why us?\\".  In fact, when we saw the movie, the only thing we kept wondering about is exactly what the alien at the prologue did and whether he knew what was happening.  I don't think that is what the filmmakers wanted.

B0040T7B2W|3.0|Released in 2010, WRITE ABOUT LOVE is the eighth album by Scottish pop band Belle and Sebastian, and the second produced by Tony Hoffer. While the production isn't so electro as on the album's predecessor of five years before, [[ASIN:B000E11568 The Life Pursuit]], Belle and Sebastian continue their evolution to more adult themes, with increasingly less of a twee pop feel.<br /><br />While Belle & Sebastian changed greatly in the new millennium, they have generally remained recognisable as the same band. What disappoints me about WRITE ABOUT LOVE is that in their tributes to certain British and international pop forebears, they efface their distinct personality entirely. \\"I Didn't See It Coming\\" and \\"I'm Not Living in the Real World\\" don't at all evoke the band I've come to love, and this phenomenon reaches its lowest depths in the horribly misguided decision to bring in Norah Jones for guest vocals in \\"Little Lou, Ugly Jack, Prophet John\\".<br /><br />Of the songs that sound like classic (or at least earlier) Belle and Sebastian, a few are generally weaker than previous material. Stuart Murdoch's lyrics have gone from twee storytelling to cryptic verse, though sometimes being unexpectedly open about his religious faith, and I'm rather baffled by \\"Calculating Bimbo\\", \\"The Ghost of Rockschool\\" and \\"Read the Blessed Pages\\". However, what redeems this album for me and brings it up to three stars is the inclusion of \\"I Want the World to Stop\\", which I'd put in my Belle and Sebastian top 10 list, \\"Sunday's Pretty Icons\\", and the fine Sarah Martin-penned \\"I Can See Your Future\\", which won over this listener who usually doesn't like when Murdoch delegates songwriting duties.<br /><br />Fans of Belle and Sebastian will want to pick this up. However, it's clear to me at least that the bulk of Stuart Murdoch's recent inspiration had gone into the superb [[ASIN:B002736YNA God Help the Girl]] album that Belle and Sebastian released as a side project the year before.

B000SEGUDE|5.0|Cloud Atlas is in one word, a wonder. It's spiritual and gorgeous in breadth. It's highly challenging in structure. It's poignant in theme--so poignant that I find my self many weeks later thinking back on its characters with a bit of loss and a bit of hope. It truly is the kind of book that you never forget, no matter how many whodunits or chillhangers you've run through at the beach or on the train. This one stays with you, because it lives in the human heart.<br /><br />Much has been made of author Mitchell's unique structure for this books, which was actually called \\"unreadable\\" by some baffled reviewer who is responsible for making nominations for the Booker Prize. Honestly, if I can read and understand this, and I have no Cambridge degree, then I'm sure that any reasonably smart person can put it together. Sure you scratch your head after chapter one ends inexplicably. But when you find that someone mentions to the letter-writer in section two that he has been reading section one as a book--and can't find the second half--well, your mind begins to go to work. Let's just say that it takes longer for some than for others to realize that Mitchell has written a literary clamshell with early history forming a coating around the ever-engrossing forays into the future. When we hit the ultimate future, we go back again to the past, meeting all the characters we met again on our way out. It's a fascinating way of telling a story and I never found it let me down, even on the way out when you'd think the story would decrease in interest. It actually worked a bit as a detective yarn and answered a number of questions that were left hanging.<br /><br />However, structure is not what makes this book so incredible. It's the way a musical composition, \\"Cloud Atlas Sextet\\" by Robert Frobisher (of the second segment) permeates the book. Whether they are aware of the composition or not, the characters are held together by music, as they are by a birthmark many of them wear. You can believe in reincarnation or not (and Mitchell even makes fun of himself for putting the belief in the book), but something like it keeps everyone and everything connected in humanity. Call it love at its deepest level, call it God's touch, call it whatever you like, something has united these characters and the music in an aural way of making the connection real.<br /><br />This is a book for people who take big issues seriously and not for those who like little tricks. I'm hoping the movie that will come out in October will also latch onto the spiritual theme.can believe in reincarnation or not (and Mitchell even makes fun of himself for putting the belief in the book), but something like it keeps everyone and everything connected in humanity. Kevin Bacon, Fred Ward and Reba McEntire head up a cast that's about as one-dimensional as it gets, flaunting their stereotypes with enthusiasm in the midst of an utterly ridiculous set of circumstances. B0083SBJXS|4.0|The culmination of Marvel's four-year, five-picture master plan is, puzzlingly, both as good as I'd hoped and a mild disappointment. It excels at playing up the larger-than-life aspects of the story, from the testy initial relationship between heroes and the looming magnitude of the threat they're called together to face. Such moments are dispersed liberally, perhaps once every five minutes, and they're an effective method of keeping the audience on the hook for the duration. What's missing, at least from where I sit, is a measure of substance or depth behind those fireworks. Robert Downey Jr. is again excellent in his nuanced interpretation of the paradoxical Tony Stark, and Mark Ruffalo might just be the best Hulk to grace the silver screen, but by comparison the rest of the cast feels quite thin, shallow and (for lack of a better word) cartoonish. Captain America, for example, wears a completely different personality than he did in his solo picture, and never feels quite right as the commanding field general he's presented to be. The most intriguing aspect of his character, an emotional detachment from modern culture, is barely even mentioned. He, and the rest of the team, feels like a suit of armor with a few good one-liners, not a living, breathing human being. It's a trap that was mostly evaded in the stars' preceding solo shots, so it's a shame to see it tripped here, especially given the amount of time and effort that was expended to define each individual's motivations. Those nitpicks certainly aren't enough to stop me from enjoying the closest thing we'll ever get to an all-splash-page crossover mega-event on the big screen, but they're also irritating enough to keep tickling the back of my mind while the next set of fuses are lit. It's a herculean effort with some truly great rewards, but not the picture-perfect ensemble masterpiece it would have you believe.

B005LAIIRQ|2.0|Filmmakers of \\"The Grey\\" should thank ever-reliable Liam Neeson for joining their project. Ottway take a flight with the crew of oil workers, but his plane crashes in the Alaskan wilderness. The premise may reminds us of \\"Alive\\" (1993), a film based on true events, but \\"The Grey,\\" directed by Joe Carnahan (\\"Narc\\" \\"The A-Team\\"), it seems, aims for something different - something more mystical.<br /><br />Thus grey wolves are depicted as very violent creatures with uncanny abilities to spot the humans. I do not disagree with the idea of turning wolves into symbolic presence, but I still don't know whether I should take the film's story seriously or not. While Joe Carnahan's approach to the subject matter of survival tends to emphasize the realism (with the spectacular visual of the freezing snow and wilderness), the story that unfolds here often looks like an action-movie template, with a group of blood-thirsty wolves (special effects included) and cliff jumping.<br /><br />Some viewers would find the film's abrupt ending (including one post-credit scene) unsatisfactory (is there an alternate ending?) Some may find the film's tone too depressing. But what I didn't like about the film is its lack of credible motives on the humans' side. Why do they have to leave the plane, for instance? Neeson's character explains, but never convinces, as his character, if not his performance, lacks the credible voice of Jack London, who would have given some advice to filmmakers.<br /><br />\\"The Grey\\" has two good things in it, Liam Neeson's acting and beautiful photography. Two stars for them.
